  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e41fddff-d014-4a62-8f38-c90f394bf0ad'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer28595128373735939"><i>Alamut</i> takes place in 11th Century Persia, in the fortress of Alamut, where self-proclaimed prophet Hasan ibn Sabbah is setting up his mad but brilliant plan to rule the region with a handful elite fighters who are to become his "living daggers." By creating a virtual paradise at Alamut, filled with beautiful women, lush gardens, wine and hashish, Sabbah is able to convinc</span>
  <span id="freeText28595128373735939" style="display:none"><i>Alamut</i> takes place in 11th Century Persia, in the fortress of Alamut, where self-proclaimed prophet Hasan ibn Sabbah is setting up his mad but brilliant plan to rule the region with a handful elite fighters who are to become his "living daggers." By creating a virtual paradise at Alamut, filled with beautiful women, lush gardens, wine and hashish, Sabbah is able to convince his young fighters that they can reach paradise if they follow his commands.<br />With parallels to Osama bin Laden, Alamut tells the story of how Sabbah was able to instill fear into the ruling class by creating a small army of devotees who were willing to kill, and be killed, in order to achieve paradise. Believing in the supreme Ismaili motto “Nothing is true, everything is permitted,” Sabbah wanted to “experiment” with how far he could manipulate religious devotion for his own political gain through appealing to what he called the stupidity and gullibility of people and their passion for pleasure and selfish desires. <br />The novel focuses on Sabbah as he unveils his plan to his inner circle, and on two of his young followers — the beautiful slave girl Halima, who has come to Alamut to join Sabbah's paradise on earth, and young ibn Tahir, Sabbah's most gifted fighter. As both Halima and ibn Tahir become disillusioned with Sabbah's vision, their lives take unexpected turns. <br /><br /><i>Alamut</i> was originally written in 1938 as an allegory to Mussolini's fascist state. In the 1960's it became a cult favorite throughout Tito's Yugoslavia, and in the 1990s, during the Balkan's War, it was read as an allegory of the region's strife and became a bestseller in Germany, France and Spain. Following the attacks of September 11, 2001, the book once again took on a new life, selling more than 20,000 copies in a new Slovenian edition, and being translated around the world in more than 19 languages. This edition, translated by Michael Biggins, in the first-ever English translation. <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="28595128373735939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/7818475">زكرياء</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">مجرد فرضية، الفرق في الترجمات العربية قد يكون في أسلوب الكتابة و في الإخلاص إلى النص الأصلي، لأن الترجمة ليس شيء دقيق و مثالي جداً، لكن هذا لا يعني أن<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">مجرد فرضية، الفرق في الترجمات العربية قد يكون في أسلوب الكتابة و في الإخلاص إلى النص الأصلي، لأن الترجمة ليس شيء دقيق و مثالي جداً، لكن هذا لا يعني أنه يوجد اختلاف كبير في المعنى، أنا أحب أن يخلص المترجم إلى النص الأصلي و أن لا يحرفه، إذا كان لا يعجبه النص أو يريد حذف أجزاء منه فلماذا يريد ترجمته من الأساس ؟؟ <br />من الممكن أن يحذف المترجم بعض المقاطع اثناء ترجمته الرواية إلى العربية، لهذا قد تختلف عدد الصفحات بين ترجمة و أخرى و هذا تصرف خاطئ جداً بالنسبة لي<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_2018844202" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2018844202" />
    <a title="James Q. Golden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48814762-james-q-golden"><img alt="James Q. Golden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553761916p2/48814762.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2018844202?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Q. Golden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Q. Golden" href="/user/show/48814762-james-q-golden">James Q. Golden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48814762-james-q-golden?shelf=haecceity">haecceity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48814762-james-q-golden?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2018844202" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer130461717833980708">I love this book beyond stars.<br/><br/>The reasons I love this book is because it shows with perfect realism how religious fanatics were developed and maintained (in this particular case, Muslims), how harems were built and worked, and how higher-ups used knowledge--or rather, the lack of knowledge about life and spirituality and human existence in general, to steer the minds of young, naive men and sustain dogma, thirsty for blood and revenge.<br/><br/>I love this book because the antagonist is cooler than the pr</span>
  <span id="freeText130461717833980708" style="display:none">I love this book beyond stars.<br /><br />The reasons I love this book is because it shows with perfect realism how religious fanatics were developed and maintained (in this particular case, Muslims), how harems were built and worked, and how higher-ups used knowledge--or rather, the lack of knowledge about life and spirituality and human existence in general, to steer the minds of young, naive men and sustain dogma, thirsty for blood and revenge.<br /><br />I love this book because the antagonist is cooler than the protagonist until the protagonist becomes the antagonist and the antagonist becomes the protagonist, which is even cooler. I love it because after a point words ending in -ist and -ism are rendered shallow and empty and meaningless. Because our hero/anti-hero fights fire with wildfire, using the same means, incorporating extreme and innovative techniques--mischievous even--to bring his own dogma to the world: the dogma of no dogma, installing it with such masterful scheming that would make Machiavelli green with envy.<br /><br />I love this book because it's about ancient Persia written by a Slovenian (which doesn't make any sense at all, if you think about it), proving that great stories have no borders and no race. Everyone laughed at Vladimir Bartol whenever he told them what he was working on; nobody believed in his work except him. It took him ten whole years to write and publish this story, but unfortunately, under the circumstances of his time, the book did not come out with a bang, not even a spark. It seemed it was destined to drift and drown into the unknown not known, having only a handful of readers. (no Internet, no party.)<br /><br />And yet it survived. Books are the planks that carry the stories they tell; and even if everything went downhill and the Titanic sank and DiCaprio died, the story survived. And like Rose it flourished. (Weird metaphor, eh?)<br /><br />Maybe. Maybe not; because a vast image of water is Exactly what you need before you plunge into the desert, before you see and feel how it's like to be trained and brainwashed into a coldblooded killer who dreams of luscious virgins and beautiful gardens waiting for him in the afterlife, eager and desperate to die with his every passing moment. Before the author so artfully switches POV and takes you backstage to show you how all this happened.<br /><br />I love this book because the real message it conveys seeps slowly through the veins of our understanding, and as we grow older and gain more experience about what is this we call our world and our reality, it resonates truer than ever.<br /><br />Nothing is true; everything is permitted.</span>
  <a data-text-id="130461717833980708" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_468393121" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/468393121" />
    <a title="Primoz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6835448-primoz"><img alt="Primoz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498656630p2/6835448.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/468393121?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Primoz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Primoz" href="/user/show/6835448-primoz">Primoz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer468393121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9242496166272873119">I read Alamut in Slovene, so I can’t really speak for the English translator’s work. I daresay it could only have improved the stylistically clumsy original. Bartol was generally a man of ideas and thought experiments, and not exactly a master of prose. The prose is awkward and uninspired, generally dull and prone to dip into cheesiness when he tries to go beyond his abilities. He’s not very good at evocative imagery, which is really a shame, given the oriental setting. Partly it’s because Slove</span>
  <span id="freeText9242496166272873119" style="display:none">I read Alamut in Slovene, so I can’t really speak for the English translator’s work. I daresay it could only have improved the stylistically clumsy original. Bartol was generally a man of ideas and thought experiments, and not exactly a master of prose. The prose is awkward and uninspired, generally dull and prone to dip into cheesiness when he tries to go beyond his abilities. He’s not very good at evocative imagery, which is really a shame, given the oriental setting. Partly it’s because Slovene is a difficult language to wield artistically, so translations may well have obscured these issues and improved on the original.<br /><br />Bartol has other weaknesses. His characterisation is uneven, for starters. He has his favourite characters whom he works out in detail, giving them rich histories and philosophies, though even then he doesn’t convince me psychologically — which is strange, since the man was trained as a psychologist, after all. The side characters are atrocious, their motivations and their dialogues juvenile, and the female cast is ridiculous, with a few rare exceptions, as with Miryam and Apama.<br /><br />The plotting is uneven. The beginning of the novel is drawn out, with lots of exposition and flimsy cuteness in Alamut’s gardens, and then the last third, which is arguably the most interesting, speeds past without really letting any of the events sink in. Retrospectively, I can’t remember a single memorable event from the entire novel. <br /><br />Where Bartol tries to shine is in the philosophising. He touches on Nietzschean and Machiavellian themes of the powerful Ubermensch who thinks himself fit to lead the masses, he explores the formation of ideology and its structure, how it fits into the psyches of people, references dozens of other philosophers… There are lengthy paragraphs dealing with all this that usually overwhelm the story itself. While these topics are certainly interesting and admirable in a work of fiction, and while the Islamist fundamentalism which it explores (without realising how relevant the matter would become later in the century) is certainly a "current" topic, if there ever was any in literature, my feelings remain reserved. All this could have been dealt with in a more elegant and subtle manner, and with greater effect. As it stands, Bartol seems to have shoehorned his own ruminations into his characters’s mouths and forced them to speak regardless of the overall story.<br /><br />All in all, I suppose the book is a worthy read as a quick historical flick with some profounder moments, though, honestly, if you’re interested in these topics, you might as well pick up Dostoevsky. You’ll be rewarded better in the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9242496166272873119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17472248291610369850" style="display:none">I really just found this book by researching a lot on the Assassin's Creed universe, and gave myself the assignment of reading it, since the first of the games should be very much built up upon the ideas in the novel. <br /><br />I must say that I was mesmerized and astonished by the book - I loved the story and I very much liked the way it was written.       <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zak" href="/user/show/68295640-zak">Zak</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68295640-zak?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2285475487" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12038110490412968752">"Not just simple folk from the masses, even the more exalted minds preferred a tangible lie to an ungraspable truth. All my attempts to enlighten individuals or groups came to nothing. Because truth, which for me stood at the summit of all values, was worthless to the rest of humanity."<br/><br/>"Viewed from the perspective of any individual, every pleasure that he feels is a real, genuine pleasure. Each of us is happy in his own way. So if the prospect of dying means happiness for someone, he'll delight </span>
  <span id="freeText12038110490412968752" style="display:none">"Not just simple folk from the masses, even the more exalted minds preferred a tangible lie to an ungraspable truth. All my attempts to enlighten individuals or groups came to nothing. Because truth, which for me stood at the summit of all values, was worthless to the rest of humanity."<br /><br />"Viewed from the perspective of any individual, every pleasure that he feels is a real, genuine pleasure. Each of us is happy in his own way. So if the prospect of dying means happiness for someone, he'll delight in death just as much as another delights in money or a woman. There are no regrets after death." <br /><br />If all our experiences are evaluated based on how we perceive them, is it wrong for leaders and prophets to 'manipulate' the masses by giving them what they want for sake of the greater good? 'Alamut' is a novel which cleverly masks moral and philosophical questions in the form of fiction. I have my own opinion on what Bartol was alluding to but, no doubt, others will have different opinions. In fact, the ideas presented here are applicable to many other situations and ideologies. Sure, there are some problems with the writing, chiefly in how some of the female characters are portrayed, but overall it was an intriguing read for me.<br /><br />Final rating: 4.5*</span>

<div id="review_1060120596" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1060120596" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1060120596?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone interested in the topic of Political Assassinations.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=southern-slav-greek-lit">southern-slav-greek-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1060120596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11193654457814242146">This stunning historical novel first published in 1938 is based on the live of Hassan-i-Sabbah (1050-1124 CE) an Ismaili who founded the Fedayeen (a.k.a. the Hashhashin, a.k.a. the Assassins) at Alamut a fortress town in what is now Iran. His group waged a war against the occupying Seljuk Turks.<br/><br/>Alamut is a study of how a charismatic leader can create a group of fanatical follows that will attempt to seize power through selected assassinations. In this novel, Hassan-i-Sabbah creates a fake heaven</span>
  <span id="freeText11193654457814242146" style="display:none">This stunning historical novel first published in 1938 is based on the live of Hassan-i-Sabbah (1050-1124 CE) an Ismaili who founded the Fedayeen (a.k.a. the Hashhashin, a.k.a. the Assassins) at Alamut a fortress town in what is now Iran. His group waged a war against the occupying Seljuk Turks.<br /><br />Alamut is a study of how a charismatic leader can create a group of fanatical follows that will attempt to seize power through selected assassinations. In this novel, Hassan-i-Sabbah creates a fake heaven at Alamut that is filled with beautiful young women who are supposed to be Houris. The young recruits are drugged. When they awake they find themselves in the fake heaven. They make love and then are put to sleep again. When they awake they depart on their missions to assassinate on which they will prove highly successful. By following the lifes of several of the fake houris and the young recruited assassins, Bartol makes it clear just what a great tragedy it is when young people are recruited into political organizations of this ilk.<br /><br />By writing this novel of a terrorist organization created in the 11th century Bartol, a citizen of Jugoslavia, hoped that he would be alerting the pubic to the sinister nature of Mussolini whose assassins in 1934 had killed King Alexander of Jugoslavia. Read this book. You may or may find parallels in today's world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11193654457814242146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_170650062" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/170650062" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170650062?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781556436819" href="/book/show/1664129.Alamut">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170650062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7372353241345546263">His fighters called the fedayeens believed that he can open for them the gates to paradise. They not only did not fear death, they sought it. They considered it a source of both honor and happiness to be sent by him on suicide missions. They believed, without any doubt, that their death will bring them to paradise, a place of eternal delights, where there are gardens with streams flowing clear as crystal, where there are choices food and drink, and where fair-limbed beautiful girls with dark eye</span>
  <span id="freeText7372353241345546263" style="display:none">His fighters called the fedayeens believed that he can open for them the gates to paradise. They not only did not fear death, they sought it. They considered it a source of both honor and happiness to be sent by him on suicide missions. They believed, without any doubt, that their death will bring them to paradise, a place of eternal delights, where there are gardens with streams flowing clear as crystal, where there are choices food and drink, and where fair-limbed beautiful girls with dark eyes shaped like almonds await them, who retain their youth and virginity no matter how many times they are made love to.<br /><br />He used to be part of the "mainstream" Muslim society. But he got disillusioned with the way faith seemed to be degenerating and the way their leaders seemed to be kowtowing to "foreign infidels." He was radicalized. He decided to leave and set himself and his followers up in a place called A. Here, he established his little kingdom. His enemies tried to wipe them out, starting the assault with heavy bombardment which at first frightened his followers but to which they later grew accustomed with. Eventually, safely hidden away from harm, they "grew inured to the crash and rumbling of (these) projectiles."<br /><br />He was an enigmatic figure. He rarely made appearances even to his own men (except to a select, trusted few). His actual whereabouts were often uncertain. He was considered by his followers as a prophet, and by his enemies as a madman and a cruel murderer. He acted as if he is not bound by any law or rule. He felt free to give seemingly contradictory edicts. Example: he forbade drinking and fornication because the Koran prohibits them; but to some of his followers, and on certain occasions, he even encouraged these acts. His motto for his group: "Nothing is real (or true) and everything is permitted."<br /><br />By the end of this novel, he "dies" but with a hope to continue living as an enduring legend.<br /><br />This character was not Osama Bin Laden, nor was his group the al Qaeda, nor was their place of operation Afghanistan. This novel, written by a Slovenian in 1938, is set in the year 1092 (almost a thousand years ago); the leader is Hasan ibn Sabbah, popularly called Sayyiduna (meaning "Our Leader"), and the place where they had their little kingdom was a fortress called Alamut. Action packed with carefully-tuned moral ambiguities which can set the reader into a delightful confusion as to who are the heroes and the villains, or who is right and who is wrong.<br /><br />Another curious case of literary foreshadowing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7372353241345546263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1643276622" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1643276622" />
    <a title="Bbrown" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6261192-bbrown"><img alt="Bbrown" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404741580p2/6261192.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1643276622?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bbrown" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bbrown" href="/user/show/6261192-bbrown">Bbrown</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1643276622" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3128551191456093551">This work by Bartol could have been half the length without losing anything, but even if the uneventful opening 2/3rds of the book were trimmed down, the work would still have unimpressive writing and uninteresting philosophy. <br/><br/>Alamut, my edition translated by Michael Biggins, at first reads like a work of young adult fiction. It has simplistic (not minimalist) prose and dialogue, the point of view male and female characters are given elevated importance for reasons that are largely not justified</span>
  <span id="freeText3128551191456093551" style="display:none">This work by Bartol could have been half the length without losing anything, but even if the uneventful opening 2/3rds of the book were trimmed down, the work would still have unimpressive writing and uninteresting philosophy. <br /><br />Alamut, my edition translated by Michael Biggins, at first reads like a work of young adult fiction. It has simplistic (not minimalist) prose and dialogue, the point of view male and female characters are given elevated importance for reasons that are largely not justified, and for the first half of the book the main characters are essentially attending school. School of some form is of course a popular setting for young adult fiction, and, as the point of view characters serve as the reader surrogate, an inflated importance of those characters magnifies the escapist fantasy at the core of much of young adult fiction. Eventually Alamut stops feeling like young adult fiction, but only because it starts dealing with subject matter you wouldn't expect to find in that genre: prostitution, religious zealotry, murders, suicides, executions, and interminable speeches recounting basic philosophical ideas. This shift doesn't make Alamut any better, largely because Bartol has nothing interesting to say about these topics and his writing does not assist in any visceral experience of the subject matter.<br /><br />From the setting of Iran, to the subject matter of fanaticization, to the Muslim faith, to the drug use of the characters, none of it struck me as something Bartol was writing with one jot of personal insight. Rather, the book smacked of Bartol having researched these things in textbooks, allowing him to be factually accurate without being able to imbue anything with any degree of emotional impact. A writer need not write only about things personally experienced in order to write something worth reading, but a writer should avoid making a work of literature feel like a thinly disguised history paper. To me, Bartol failed in this. Bartol also failed in making certain segments of this book not feel like thinly disguised philosophy papers, and very basic philosophy papers at that. The second half of this book has numerous, lengthy speeches from the character Hasan outlining his revelations and beliefs, and they boil down to some basics of nihilism, existentialism, and (most commonly) subjectivism &amp; relativism. If you know anything about any of these philosophical ideas, Alamut will have nothing new to offer you. <br /><br />This lack of anything interesting to say about the subject matter of the book, and lack of interesting ideas presented, are Alamut's largest flaws, but there are numerous additional flaws as well. For one thing, the book is longer than it should be. The point of Hasan having invented a fake heaven is evident from before even a quarter of the book has passed, but the first visit to this fake heaven is only completed at the 2/3rd mark of the book. The book is 3/4ths over by the time the first assassination occurs. There is far too much of Hasan knowingly smiling and saying "wait until you see the surprises I’ve got in store!" even after any reader paying attention knows exactly what those surprises are. The final fifty pages, in contrast, feel almost overly busy by comparison. Another flaw is that ibn Tahir, who can apparently be talked out of his deepest convictions by anyone in about five minutes, makes for an unsympathetic and irritating protagonist. Yet another flaw is that the poetry featured in this book is terrible, though that may be on Biggins. Certainly, whoever is responsible, the writing is at times just bad: at one point the book reads "[n]ow they’re in need and appealing to me again. And despite the fact that they broke their word, I’ll jump to their aid once again. But tell them to think carefully next time before disappointing me again." Three sentences in a row all ending in "again" is poor writing, plain and simple. This book about suicidal assassins has repeated instances where moths burn up from flying into flames, the type of too on-the-nose metaphor that really rubs me the wrong way. There are other problems, but you get the gist.<br /><br />In theory this book seemed like something I would like, an exotic setting and an interesting central concept, with warfare and political intrigue but also a deep exploration of characters so devoted to an idea and a man that they were willing to sacrifice themselves. Unfortunately, Alamut failed to execute any of this in an interesting way. It is overlong, all of it reads as far more sterile than it should, and it's uninteresting to boot. Despite all these negatives, however, it was evident that Bartol was trying to do something interesting with this book. 3/5 stars for that.<br /><br />According to the intro/afterwards there has been a revival in interest for this book following 9/11. For people interested in suicide terrorism I would point you toward Dying to Win by Robert Pape, a nonfiction book that evinces an actual understanding for the subject that Alamut completely lacks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3128551191456093551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1265332433" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1265332433" />
    <a title="Ali Berdai" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40990383-ali-berdai"><img alt="Ali Berdai" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463210172p2/40990383.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1265332433?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ali Berdai" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ali Berdai" href="/user/show/40990383-ali-berdai">Ali Berdai</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1265332433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9902026299084348939">Wonderful piece of literature, astonishingly avant-guard. Although written in the first quarter of the twentieth century, it appears to be more relevant than ever in a period dominated by terrorism. The book throws the reader into the mind of a megalomaniac plotting against his archenemies using a brainwashed army willing to die to serve the Ismaili cause. Major philosophical ideas and concepts captivate the reader all throughout the book, and even after the end.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_17896146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17896146" />
    <a title="Souldaddy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/997616-souldaddy"><img alt="Souldaddy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206983071p2/997616.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17896146?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Souldaddy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Souldaddy" href="/user/show/997616-souldaddy">Souldaddy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781556436819" href="/book/show/1664129.Alamut">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of Assassin's Creed</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17896146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer451300150886794544">I read this book as a companion to Assassin's Creed, the setting in that game is so rich. Alamut takes the basic legends surrounding the Assassins (you can find most of them in Wikipedia) and breathes life into those legends. Many contradictions are explored - how could the Assassins have been deeply ascetic and pious and yet smoke hashish and preach "nothing is true, everything is permitted"? In answering these questions, Alamut chronicles the creations of a religion not unlike Scientology, whe</span>
  <span id="freeText451300150886794544" style="display:none">I read this book as a companion to Assassin's Creed, the setting in that game is so rich. Alamut takes the basic legends surrounding the Assassins (you can find most of them in Wikipedia) and breathes life into those legends. Many contradictions are explored - how could the Assassins have been deeply ascetic and pious and yet smoke hashish and preach "nothing is true, everything is permitted"? In answering these questions, Alamut chronicles the creations of a religion not unlike Scientology, where blind faith and dedication are manipulated for selfish ends. We follow the creation of the first assassins, and their initiation rite where the young men are drugged and tricked into thinking they have ascended into the afterlife of the Koran, a garden paradise filled with wine and virgins. The young fedayeen (elite assassins) then return to the normal world with such longing that they willingly throw their lives away. The book also follows the harem girls who live as caged princesses, constantly competing among themselves and eventually meeting tragic ends when they meet the fedayeen for a single day, fall in love, then are heart broken to learn they can never see their lovers again. <br /><br />The book's weakness lies in the main "villian" Hasan ibn Sabbah. Hasan's philosophy which drives everything is a mix of Nietzschean nihilism and will to power - basically all religions are false because true knowledge is impossible to realize, mankind is enslaved by itself own desire for fantasy and falsehood, and great men rise above this to rule over the fates of others. I didn't judge Sabbah in anyway, but the author gets rather preachy when expressing the philosophy, and some sections drag on and on. The game has a much more elegant expression of these ideas.<br /><br />In the end, I really enjoyed this book, and recommend it to anyone interested in the subject.</span>
  <a data-text-id="451300150886794544" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_270620752" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/270620752" />
    <a title="Vasha7" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3850379-vasha7"><img alt="Vasha7" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/270620752?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vasha7" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vasha7" href="/user/show/3850379-vasha7">Vasha7</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781556436819" href="/book/show/1664129.Alamut">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer270620752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4858526431491067142">Most of the promotional material for this historical novel has centered on claiming it as some sort of key to modern Islamic extremism, even calling it a "training manual" for al-Qaeda! No way—these thousand-year-old events are about as relevant to the current century as the 11th-century <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Investiture_Controversy' rel='nofollow noopener'>Investiture Controversy</a> is to understanding the relationship between the Catholic Church and modern European governments. Rather, like in many historical novels, the depiction of that time period serves as a cas</span>
  <span id="freeText4858526431491067142" style="display:none">Most of the promotional material for this historical novel has centered on claiming it as some sort of key to modern Islamic extremism, even calling it a "training manual" for al-Qaeda! No way—these thousand-year-old events are about as relevant to the current century as the 11th-century <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Investiture_Controversy" rel="nofollow noopener">Investiture Controversy</a> is to understanding the relationship between the Catholic Church and modern European governments. Rather, like in many historical novels, the depiction of that time period serves as a case study and framework for more general ideas. Morality, religious belief, and the limits of freedom and manipulation all get examined here, but these are things that humans are bound to debate over and over, and different times shed different light on them. <br /><br />I will say that the book didn't start very promising. I don't think I would have gotten past the first three chapters if I hadn't been determined to finish and review it. I would have really missed something if I had stopped at that point, though. Sure, the blatant sexism of the plotline that follows the women in the story doesn't get better; but partway through we are introduced to Hasan Ibn Sabbah himself, the leader of Alamut, and the more the book focuses on him, the more interesting it gets. He is a profoundly ambiguous figure, definitely immoral but also a deep thinker, and worth listening to for the lingering, troubling ideas he raises.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4858526431491067142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_147583327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/147583327" />
    <a title="Spencer Ellsworth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4965745-spencer-ellsworth"><img alt="Spencer Ellsworth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476310411p2/4965745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/147583327?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Spencer Ellsworth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Spencer Ellsworth" href="/user/show/4965745-spencer-ellsworth">Spencer Ellsworth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer147583327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8979799862662989960">Really interesting read. A tale of the Ismaili Assassins written in Yugoslavia in the 30s, under the encroaching fascist dictatorships of Italy and Germany.<br/><br/>This book takes Marco Polo's legend of the Old Man of the Mountain and treats it seriously. The commander of the Isamili assassin group at the castle of Alamut has prepared a "heavenly garden" full of courtesans and delicacies. He sends his finest recruits their under heavy sedation, making them believe that he has sent them to Paradise. When</span>
  <span id="freeText8979799862662989960" style="display:none">Really interesting read. A tale of the Ismaili Assassins written in Yugoslavia in the 30s, under the encroaching fascist dictatorships of Italy and Germany.<br /><br />This book takes Marco Polo's legend of the Old Man of the Mountain and treats it seriously. The commander of the Isamili assassin group at the castle of Alamut has prepared a "heavenly garden" full of courtesans and delicacies. He sends his finest recruits their under heavy sedation, making them believe that he has sent them to Paradise. When the courtesans sedate them again and they awake, they believe they can go back to Paradise as soon as they die in the service of the Old Man.<br /><br />This is one of the most thought-provoking books I've ever read. The writing comes off as clumsy and overexpository in places, but it isn't enough to detract from the stunning way that Bartol deals with these heavy concepts. Each character struggles with aspects of belief and love, and each one tends to believe too hard or love too hard except the Old Man, who manipulates them with a strange kind of compassion.<br /><br />I didn't like the main character's resolution as much as I thought. [SPOILER] His loss of faith and conversion to moral relativism felt a bit forced, when he had been so passionate before. This is a profund, thought-provoking, and soon-to-be-classic work of world literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8979799862662989960" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1362842449" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1362842449" />
    <a title="Ekansh Gupta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17741169-ekansh-gupta"><img alt="Ekansh Gupta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575176853p2/17741169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1362842449?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ekansh Gupta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ekansh Gupta" href="/user/show/17741169-ekansh-gupta">Ekansh Gupta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1362842449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2950817028687947857">I read the translated version (English) and not the Slovenian one, hence was at the mercy of the translator to provide literary ups and downs. The language was not scholarly or formal which I feel was necessary to avoid letting the theme and story languish in a corner behind the writing style. The message was to be delivered to the reader, however naive he could be. Perhaps the intention was to let a layman connect with the plot.<br/><br/>I picked it up because it dealt with the subject of 'Ḥashshāshīns'.</span>
  <span id="freeText2950817028687947857" style="display:none">I read the translated version (English) and not the Slovenian one, hence was at the mercy of the translator to provide literary ups and downs. The language was not scholarly or formal which I feel was necessary to avoid letting the theme and story languish in a corner behind the writing style. The message was to be delivered to the reader, however naive he could be. Perhaps the intention was to let a layman connect with the plot.<br /><br />I picked it up because it dealt with the subject of 'Ḥashshāshīns'. I was naturally curious to be able to draw a line between fact and fiction regarding the existing knowledge I had about their history.<br /><br />Being a student of psychology myself, I appreciate the questions Alamut raised, spoken from behind the veil worn by Ibn Sabbah, the 60 year old protagonist. Hassan Ibn Sabbah, the 'old man of the mountain' was and is still considered to be an immense figure in medieval Iranian history. His journey was a brave one, from spiritual rags to military riches. Portrayed as a cunning, lean and dignified man with searching eyes and a respectful beard, he became an epitome of living without rules. Over the years of his frustrating search for the Truth, he mastered the art of operating in the gray areas where morality and deception clashed. A villainous hero, he raised himself to the stature of a demigod.<br /><br />It's sometimes hard not to fall in love with Ibn Sabbah, while detesting him at the same time. The fortress of Alamut, the 'eagle's nest', housed Hassan and his trusted deputies, many of whom didn't have any idea of what was about to happen. <br /><br />The other main characters in the book aren't anywhere near as powerful or charismatic as Ibn Sabbah, which disappoints. Ibn Tahir, a youth, portrays a stereo-typically inquisitive and wise student of Hassan while Halima, a childlike teenager and a soon-to-be 'Houris', binds the reader with her charm. Bartol was decidedly not perturbed by the needs of marketability to afford too much strength to his younger protagonists, as a result sticking with the freedom that they would have been given historically. It makes them, on occasions, seem helpless which is fine by the way, for they are but cogs in an elaborate plan.<br /><br />When Ibn Sabbah's plan is unmasked, it provides both of them with a philosophical make-or-break situation, which Ibn tahir makes good of but Halima, too naive to appreciate the tidings, meets a tragic end.<br /><br />Unsurprisingly, similarities between Bin Laden and Ibn Sabbah stemmed after this book was released which would also explain the sky-rocketing sales of this book post the 9/11 calamity. The Ismaeli motto: 'Nothing is true, everything is permitted' certainly helped Ibn Sabbah to reiterate in his defense that either there is no God or that He does not care. <br /><br />Does this make him get away with the brazen brutality he displayed? No, and yes. What Hassan wanted us to examine was our propensity to believe in common fictions (which include morals, ethics, human rights et al) as a species.<br /><br />Perhaps it wouldn't be so ironic to call him an 'atheist prophet' when this book mostly deals with the contrast between what people have chosen to believe in and what Hassan Ibn Sabbah proudly cherished and proclaimed, standing atop his tower, while giving orders to novices to perform a 'leap of faith'.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2950817028687947857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1362842449">

<div id="review_2329881935" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2329881935" />
    <a title="Aqsa " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35014606-aqsa"><img alt="Aqsa " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505323193p2/35014606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2329881935?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aqsa " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aqsa " href="/user/show/35014606-aqsa">Aqsa </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781556436819" href="/book/show/1664129.Alamut">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35014606-aqsa?shelf=adulthood">adulthood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35014606-aqsa?shelf=covers">covers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2329881935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3772668933175542380">This review might be a little long, since it gave me a lot to think about...<br/><br/>This book was based on the story of Hasan-e Sabbah, who founded the order of Assassins at the fortress of Alamut. He supposedly tricked them into thinking he could send them to paradise, by building a fake paradise to make them devout believers, ready to carry out political assassinations and further his cause. <br/><br/>When I first saw the synopsis, it was hard to believe that this was historically based rather than pure fiction</span>
  <span id="freeText3772668933175542380" style="display:none">This review might be a little long, since it gave me a lot to think about...<br /><br />This book was based on the story of Hasan-e Sabbah, who founded the order of Assassins at the fortress of Alamut. He supposedly tricked them into thinking he could send them to paradise, by building a fake paradise to make them devout believers, ready to carry out political assassinations and further his cause. <br /><br />When I first saw the synopsis, it was hard to believe that this was historically based rather than pure fiction, but it’s also very relevant to the dictatorships seen in history, and of course to modern terrorism. Hasan leads these men to their death in the name of his cause, but the most interesting part of the story (besides the fake paradise, because that is seriously out there) is the fact that he began this whole scheme after discovering that there is no ultimate god or religion, or anything waiting for him after death. <br /><br />Hasan convinces himself he’s doing these men a favour, since they go to their deaths with joy in the knowledge that they’ll go straight to paradise, while he suffers from the loneliness of knowing there is nothing out there. I wonder whether he really believes in this justification, or if he needed a distraction from that realisation. <br /><br />I think Bartol’s structure was really well done - you read from the perspective of one of the fedayeen (assassins, in this context, but literally meaning those willing to sacrifice themselves) but also from one of the girls brought to the harem. Hasan uses this harem and their hidden gardens to fool a few chosen ones into believing that he holds the key to paradise, from which the rumours propagate until everyone at Alamut is in awe. It’s a little sad how things end from the girls’ side of the story, but I know it doesn’t make sense to expect a happy ending, or happy anything, especially when Halima is so young and naive. <br /><br />Later, as both sides are trained and the military side of things progresses, we meet Hasan and see for ourselves what kind of person he is. Bartol writes him from various angles, so we get to see moments of weakness where he recognises the immorality of what he is doing, or fears that everything he has worked for will fail. In one memorable scene, he is being pulled down a pulley system by the eunuchs that guard him, and he thinks about what would happen if they realised how much power they have in that moment, if they just let go. His character is undoubtedly a distasteful and horrific one, but these moments make the story so much stronger, in my opinion. <br /><br />Seeing that one of Slovenia’s most famous books is about a political and ideological struggle in 11th century Iran is pretty epic. I appreciated not seeing a bias towards certain characters or against the events that occurred. This was a powerful book, and it explored concepts like faith, religion and nihilism, but still had interesting and complex characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3772668933175542380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2240363385" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2240363385" />
    <a title="Petra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75935783-petra"><img alt="Petra" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2240363385?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petra" href="/user/show/75935783-petra">Petra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2240363385" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11995040058056030237">I read this book in Slovene - as it was originally written - and I truly and utterly disliked it. <br/>First of many problems that Bartol has in this book is the style in which it is written. (At least in Slovene) the writing style is simple and often repetitive. Bartol uses both simple grammar and simple vocabulary. Slovene is a rich and expressive language which simply is not seen in Bartol's use of it. It is dull and often feels unnatural. <br/><br/>Secondly, the characters are 1. two-dimensional, 2. someti</span>
  <span id="freeText11995040058056030237" style="display:none">I read this book in Slovene - as it was originally written - and I truly and utterly disliked it. <br />First of many problems that Bartol has in this book is the style in which it is written. (At least in Slovene) the writing style is simple and often repetitive. Bartol uses both simple grammar and simple vocabulary. Slovene is a rich and expressive language which simply is not seen in Bartol's use of it. It is dull and often feels unnatural. <br /><br />Secondly, the characters are 1. two-dimensional, 2. sometimes feel bipolar (especially the women) and 3. often lack motivation for their actions. For example Jusuf and Sulejman's characters are defined by a single characteristic, Halima is written as if she has absolutely no control over her emotions and unexplainably changes her mood multiple times over the course of a few pages. Finally <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Ibn Tahir is able to shake his illusions (for which he was prepared to give his life) only due to a few words from the opposite party, only to be completely filled by fuming rage - which inexplicably vanishes and is forgotten as soon as Hassan explains his plan in a short speech  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Both transitions lack motivation and are written in a quick and unconvincing manner. I mean, could a believable character truly abandon all his believes (not once, but twice in a few chapters) just due to a speech made by his enemy?<br /><br />Thirdly, I think Bartol gets a bit lost trying to juggle two contradicting philosophies in a single book. On one hand you will find the elements of Plato's philosophy (the structure of hierarchy, Ibn Tahir's leaving in search of knowledge ...), on the other hand the book also follows a lot of Nietzsche's ideas (idea of the overman and overcoming 'slave morality' - although Ibn Sabah fails at it...). The philosophies are slightly contradicting as Plato's promotes absolute knowledge of goodness and Nietzsche's promotes absolute relativity. So the reader could justifiably be confused as to which philosophy the book promotes. Other than that Ibn Sabah never truly accomplishes to abandon religion and although Hassan does employ some of Nietzsche's ideas he also quotes Zarathustra's shadow and therefore conveys ideas opposite to Nietzsche's philosophy. In short, philosophy wise the book is rather confusing. <br /><br />Finally, I would like to mention that this book was not written as a critique of Muslim extremism although it is often interpreted in that way. It would be more accurate to say that the book was written as a critique of any dictatorship and manipulation of the masses, but was written as the direct response to the Italian Fascist regime, that was at that time oppressing the Slovene nation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11995040058056030237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_167376974" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/167376974" />
    <a title="Eveningstar2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4450727-eveningstar2"><img alt="Eveningstar2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541882452p2/4450727.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/167376974?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eveningstar2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eveningstar2" href="/user/show/4450727-eveningstar2">Eveningstar2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer167376974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18126898945327685650">European literature circa World War II tends to be aggressively tragic and grapple with nihilist/existentialist themes. Bartol's Alamut is no different. This is not a story about characters so much as forces: historical, social, or--in the case of Alamut's hyper-rationalist and sociopathic master Hasan ibn Sabbah--dogmatic.<br/><br/>It's impossible to talk about Alamut without first discussing Hasan ibn Sabbah (or Hassan i-Sabbah), the master of the Ismaili sect and prophet of its notoriously crypto-anarc</span>
  <span id="freeText18126898945327685650" style="display:none">European literature circa World War II tends to be aggressively tragic and grapple with nihilist/existentialist themes. Bartol's Alamut is no different. This is not a story about characters so much as forces: historical, social, or--in the case of Alamut's hyper-rationalist and sociopathic master Hasan ibn Sabbah--dogmatic.<br /><br />It's impossible to talk about Alamut without first discussing Hasan ibn Sabbah (or Hassan i-Sabbah), the master of the Ismaili sect and prophet of its notoriously crypto-anarchic motto: "Nothing is true; everything is permitted." Most of the story revolves around Hasan and the execution of a grand experiment through which he asserts his grim motto. <br /><br />To wit, the entire novel is the narration of Hasan's ironic experiment, his belief in a meaningless universe and an uncaring God, and his subsequent infliction of atrocities in an attempt to prove his theory by negation. He is a manipulator, a sociopath who gambles with lives, all the while awaiting some divine judgement to strike him down and prove him wrong.<br /><br />There's something subtly ironic about Hasan ibn Sabbah. Despite his obvious centrality to the novel and its eponymous fortress, I could never shake off a persistent feeling that Bartol undermined Hasan's validity. We are never quite sure if Hasan ibn Sabbah and the Ismaili motto are correct, or merely misunderstood, or inherently paradoxical. Despite his claims toward prophetic power and universal knowledge, Hasan is a fragile, idiosyncratic, jealous and isolated old man. <br /><br />And this depiction truly owes itself to Bartol's writing style (or, more accurately, Michael Biggins's translation). Bartol writes with emotional distance. His language is clear, objective and clinical. Sympathetic characters shine through by their own virtue, rather than authorial insistence. Miriam, Halima, Yusuf, ibn Tahir and Suleiman appeal to the reader by their own actions, their beliefs and their emotions. There is something admirably understated about Bartol's writing.<br /><br />And yet, I could not help but feel he erred too strongly on the side of minimalism. Sometimes entire chapters lacked pathos. There is a certain feeling of emptiness in reading Alamut. A lack of denouement and an absence of any real heroic characters on whom to cling. And, honestly, that sort of emptiness seems fitting for a story like this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18126898945327685650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17520589910407481172" style="display:none">Some ideas are normal. Some are incredible. Some are pure and some are so evil. <tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1222241126">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_849640122" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/849640122" />
    <a title="Kristel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1993309-kristel"><img alt="Kristel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569870309p2/1993309.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/849640122?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristel" href="/user/show/1993309-kristel">Kristel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781556436819" href="/book/show/1664129.Alamut">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1993309-kristel?shelf=1001-challenge">1001-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1993309-kristel?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer849640122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5979258453028822035">Tags/Categories: 1001 Books, Slovenian literature, Iran, Ismaili, <br/><br/>This story is of Alamut and Hasan ibn al-Sabbah is set in 1092 in Iran and is the story of Hasan and his rise to power and control through young men trained to be killers willing to risk their lives for paradise. <br/><br/>Supposedly this is an allegory of Fascism. It was written in 1938 as forces were grabbing lands of the Slovenian people. Regardless, in the words of Michael Biggins “Alamut was and is simply a great read--imaginative, eru</span>
  <span id="freeText5979258453028822035" style="display:none">Tags/Categories: 1001 Books, Slovenian literature, Iran, Ismaili, <br /><br />This story is of Alamut and Hasan ibn al-Sabbah is set in 1092 in Iran and is the story of Hasan and his rise to power and control through young men trained to be killers willing to risk their lives for paradise. <br /><br />Supposedly this is an allegory of Fascism. It was written in 1938 as forces were grabbing lands of the Slovenian people. Regardless, in the words of Michael Biggins “Alamut was and is simply a great read--imaginative, erudite, dynamic and humorous, a well-told tale set in an exotic time and place, yet populated by characters with universally recognizable ambitions, dreams and imperfections.” I couldn’t say it any better. I loved this story. <br /><br />“Nothing is true. Everything is permitted.” The supreme Ismaili Motto, could very well be the motto that reflects our current times. This Hasan used anything and everything to gain his desire. He told them whatever they wanted to hear, “Because long experience has shown that men hold tightly to whatever they’ve invested their money in.” Hasan had believed there was no truth. He felt free to create a truth that served his purpose and he felt no remorse for using others for his gain. When we believe no truth then everything we want is permissible. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5979258453028822035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2503833234" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2503833234" />
    <a title="Goran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83996359-goran"><img alt="Goran" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2503833234?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Goran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Goran" href="/user/show/83996359-goran">Goran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83996359-goran?shelf=foreign-cultures">foreign-cultures</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83996359-goran?shelf=history-fiction">history-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83996359-goran?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2503833234" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14682094051963298045">Alamut is a novel about Hasan ibn Sabbah, the founder of the assassin sect. The assassins were called „history's first terrorists“ by some, based on their methods of warfare. The Alamut, itself, was a fortress and headquarter of Hasan ibn Sabbah and his sect - Ismailis.<br/><br/>In this novel we follow the lives of fedayeens (elite unite of Alamut's forces) and girls who were living in heavenly gardens in Alamut. Hasan tricked his „fedayeens“ with combination of hashish, complete obedience and heavenly ga</span>
  <span id="freeText14682094051963298045" style="display:none">Alamut is a novel about Hasan ibn Sabbah, the founder of the assassin sect. The assassins were called „history's first terrorists“ by some, based on their methods of warfare. The Alamut, itself, was a fortress and headquarter of Hasan ibn Sabbah and his sect - Ismailis.<br /><br />In this novel we follow the lives of fedayeens (elite unite of Alamut's forces) and girls who were living in heavenly gardens in Alamut. Hasan tricked his „fedayeens“ with combination of hashish, complete obedience and heavenly gardens, and fedayeens believed they were in paradise. For the chance to go back to paradise, they were willing to do everything, even to sacrifice their lives. <br />They became a fearful force for their ability to kill anybody. Even the mighty Sultan of Seljuk empire was afraid of this sect.<br /><br />This book gives us deeper understanding of Hasan's views on faith, religion and his long-term goals, as well as political/historical background that played a role in foundation of assassin's units. Alamut is the most famous book written by Vladimir Bartol, and also one of the most important novels in Slovenian literature. Also, this book served as inspiration for famous video game: „Assassin's Creed“.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14682094051963298045" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1878801821" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1878801821" />
    <a title="Gohar Khokhar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39033898-gohar-khokhar"><img alt="Gohar Khokhar" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1878801821?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gohar Khokhar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gohar Khokhar" href="/user/show/39033898-gohar-khokhar">Gohar Khokhar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1878801821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16575753643878071526">Alamut is about Hassan ibn Sabbah, his heaven on earth and his Assassin. Without considering historical or religious authenticity it is an interesting story, where a leader though his determination and strong will realized his dream of bringing down the Seljuk empire. Through strength of his personality he managed to influenced the mind of his followers to the extent that they are willing to die for him. <br/><br/>Discussion between Hassan and his two trusted men i.e. Buzurg Umeed and Abu Ali is thoughtfu</span>
  <span id="freeText16575753643878071526" style="display:none">Alamut is about Hassan ibn Sabbah, his heaven on earth and his Assassin. Without considering historical or religious authenticity it is an interesting story, where a leader though his determination and strong will realized his dream of bringing down the Seljuk empire. Through strength of his personality he managed to influenced the mind of his followers to the extent that they are willing to die for him. <br /><br />Discussion between Hassan and his two trusted men i.e. Buzurg Umeed and Abu Ali is thoughtful. I felt description of heaven and hor, and their encounter with Fedayeen was not very powerful, it could have been more persuasive or may be it is due to translation. <br /><br />Overall it is a good read, language is simple and with little knowledge of Islamic History you can enjoy it more and can visualize characters.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16575753643878071526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
